<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Māori people</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Māori people of New Zealand. For the Māori people of the Cook Islands, see <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islanders" title="Cook Islanders">Cook Islanders</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Maouri_people" title="Maouri people">Maouri people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauri_people" title="Mauri people">Mauri people</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Moriori" title="Moriori">Moriori</a>.</div>

<p>The <b>Māori</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>Māori pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/M%C4%81ori" title="Help:IPA/Māori">[ˈmaːɔɾi]</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Rar-M%C4%81ori.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/Rar-M%C4%81ori.ogg" title="Rar-Māori.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>) are the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous</a> <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesian people</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. Māori originated with settlers from eastern <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a>, who arrived in New Zealand in several waves of canoe voyages some time between 1250 and 1300.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Over several centuries in isolation, the Polynesian settlers developed <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_culture" title="Māori culture">a unique culture</a>, with their own language, a rich mythology, and distinctive crafts and performing arts. Early Māori formed tribal groups based on eastern Polynesian social customs and organisation. Horticulture flourished using plants they introduced; later, a prominent <a href="/wiki/Warrior" title="Warrior">warrior</a> culture emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The arrival of Europeans to New Zealand, starting in the 17th century, brought enormous changes to the Māori way of life. Māori people gradually adopted many aspects of Western society and culture. Initial relations between Māori and Europeans were largely amicable, and with the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi" title="Treaty of Waitangi">Treaty of Waitangi</a> in 1840, the two cultures coexisted as part of a new British colony. Rising tensions over disputed land sales led to conflict in the 1860s. Social upheaval, decades of conflict and epidemics of introduced disease took a devastating toll on the Māori population, which fell dramatically. By the start of the 20th century, the Māori population had begun to recover, and efforts have been made to increase their standing in wider New Zealand society and achieve social justice. Traditional Māori culture has thereby enjoyed a significant revival, which was further bolstered by a <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_protest_movement" title="Māori protest movement">Māori protest movement</a> that emerged in the 1960s.
</p><p>In the 2013 census, there were approximately 600,000 people in New Zealand identifying as Māori, making up roughly 15 per cent of the national population. They are the second-largest ethnic group in New Zealand, after European New Zealanders ("<span lang="en" title="English language text"><a href="/wiki/P%C4%81keh%C4%81" title="Pākehā">Pākehā</a></span>"). In addition, more than 140,000 Māori live in Australia. The <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="Māori language">Māori language</a> is spoken to some extent by about a fifth of all Māori, representing 3 per cent of the total population. Māori are active in all spheres of New Zealand culture and society, with independent representation in areas such as media, politics and sport.
</p><p>Disproportionate numbers of Māori face significant economic and social obstacles, and generally have lower life expectancies and incomes compared with other New Zealand ethnic groups. They suffer higher levels of crime, health problems, and educational under-achievement. A number of socioeconomic initiatives have been instigated with the aim of "closing the gap" between Māori and other New Zealanders. Political and economic redress for historical grievances is also ongoing (see <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi_claims_and_settlements" title="Treaty of Waitangi claims and settlements">Treaty of Waitangi claims and settlements</a>).
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Language"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Language</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/60px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/80px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Look up <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/M%C4%81ori" title="wiktionary:Māori">Māori</a></b></i> in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="Māori language">Māori language</a>, the word <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">māori</i> means "normal", "natural" or "ordinary". In legends and oral traditions, the word distinguished ordinary mortal human beings—<i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">tāngata māori</i>—from <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_religion" title="Māori religion">deities</a> and spirits (<i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">wairua</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_Notei"><a href="#endnote_Notei">[i]</a></sup> Likewise, <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">wai māori</i> denotes "fresh water", as opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_water" title="Salt water">salt water</a>. There are <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> words in most <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian languages</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> all deriving from Proto-Polynesian <i>*ma(a)qoli</i>, which has the reconstructed meaning "true, real, genuine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollex_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-pollex-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eastern_Polynesian_Languages_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eastern_Polynesian_Languages-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spelling of "Māori" with or without the <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">macron</a> is inconsistent in general-interest English-language media in New Zealand,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> although some newspapers and websites have adopted the standard Māori-language spelling (i.e., with macrons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_and_self-naming">Naming and self-naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Naming and self-naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Early visitors from Europe to New Zealand generally referred to the indigenous inhabitants as "New Zealanders" or as "natives".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The Māori used the term <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">Māori</i> to describe themselves in a pan-tribal sense.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Noteii"><a href="#endnote_Noteii">[ii]</a></sup> Māori people often use the term <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Tangata_whenua" title="Tangata whenua">tangata whenua</a></i> (literally, "people of the land") to identify in a way that expresses their relationship with a particular area of land; a tribe may be the <i>tangata whenua</i> in one area, but not in another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The term can also refer to the Māori people as a whole in relation to New Zealand (<i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Aotearoa" title="Aotearoa">Aotearoa</a></i>) as a whole.
</p><p>The Maori Purposes Act of 1947 required the use of the term "Māori" rather than "Native" in official usage. The Department of Native Affairs was renamed as the Department of Māori Affairs; since 1992 it has been known as <span lang="mi" style="font-style:normal;" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Te_Puni_K%C5%8Dkiri" title="Te Puni Kōkiri">Te Puni Kōkiri</a></span>, or the Ministry for Māori Development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Before 1974, the government required documented ancestry to determine the legal definition of "a Māori person". For example, bloodlines or percentage of Māori ancestry was used to determine whether a person should enroll on the general electoral roll or the separate <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_electorates" title="Māori electorates">Māori roll</a>. In 1947, the authorities determined that a man who was five-eighths Māori had improperly voted in the general parliamentary electorate of <a href="/wiki/Raglan_(New_Zealand_electorate)" title="Raglan (New Zealand electorate)">Raglan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Maori Affairs Amendment Act 1974 changed the definition, allowing individuals to self-identify as to their cultural identity. In matters involving financial benefits provided by the government to people of Māori ethnicity—scholarships, for example, or <a href="/wiki/Waitangi_Tribunal" title="Waitangi Tribunal">Waitangi Tribunal</a> settlements—authorities generally require some documentation of ancestry or continuing cultural connection (such as acceptance by others as being of the people) but no minimum "blood" requirement exists as determined by the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_Noteiii"><a href="#endnote_Noteiii">[iii]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polynesian_Migration.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="553" data-file-width="553" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Polynesian_Migration.svg/350px-Polynesian_Migration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Polynesian_Migration.svg/525px-Polynesian_Migration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Polynesian_Migration.svg/700px-Polynesian_Migration.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polynesian_Migration.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Māori settlement of New Zealand represents an end-point of a long chain of <a href="/wiki/Island_hopping" title="Island hopping">island-hopping</a> voyages in the <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">South Pacific</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The most current reliable evidence strongly indicates that the initial settlement of New Zealand occurred around 1280 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">medieval warm period</a>. Previous dating of some <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiore" title="Kiore">kiore</a></i> (Polynesian rat) bones at 50–150  has now been shown to have been unreliable; new samples of bone (and now also of unequivocally rat-gnawed woody seed cases) match the 1280 date of the earliest archaeological sites and the beginning of sustained deforestation by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe-21">[21]</a></sup> Māori oral history describes the arrival of ancestors from <i><a href="/wiki/Hawaiki" title="Hawaiki">Hawaiki</a></i> (the mythical homeland in tropical Polynesia), in large ocean-going <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Waka_(canoe)" title="Waka (canoe)">waka</a></i>. Migration accounts vary among tribes (<i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Iwi" title="Iwi">iwi</a></i>), whose members may identify with several waka in their genealogies (<i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Whakapapa" title="Whakapapa">whakapapa</a></i>). In the last few decades, <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial-DNA</a> (mtDNA) research has allowed an estimate to be made of the number of women in the founding population—between 50 and 100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence from archaeology, linguistics, and physical anthropology indicates that the first settlers came from east Polynesia and became the Māori. <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_linguistics" title="Evolutionary linguistics">Language-evolution</a> studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuff.co.nz_807540_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stuff.co.nz_807540-24">[24]</a></sup> and mtDNA evidence<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> suggest that most Pacific populations originated from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taiwanese_aborigines" title="Taiwanese aborigines">Taiwanese aborigines</a> around 5,200 years ago (suggesting prior migration from the Asian or Chinese mainland).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> These ancestors moved down through <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NZ_Herald_144542_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NZ_Herald_144542-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Atholl Anderson concluded from analysis of mtDNA (female) and Y chromosome (male) that the ancestors of Polynesian women came from Taiwan while those of Polynesian men came from New Guinea. Subsequently it was found that 96 per cent of Polynesian mtDNA has an Asian origin, as do one-third of Polynesian Y chromosomes, with the remaining two-thirds being from <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> and nearby islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> An Otago University study by Professor Matisoo-Smith shows that New Zealand was populated from southern Asia with the mtDNA mainly M branch with some N lineage and Denisovan DNA. Most Polynesians, Māori included, have mtDNA in the B4a1a branch, and the founding population is now known to have been in the hundreds—much larger than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Archaic_period_.281280.E2.80.931500.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaic_period_(1280–1500)">Archaic period (1280–1500)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Archaic period (1280–1500)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest period of Māori settlement is known as the "Archaic", "Moahunter" or "Colonisation" period. The eastern Polynesian ancestors of the Māori arrived in a forested land with <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_New_Zealand" title="Birds of New Zealand">abundant birdlife</a>, including several now extinct <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">moa</a></i> species weighing between 20 kilograms (44 lb) and 250 kg (550 lb) each. Other species, also now extinct, included a swan, a goose and the giant <a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast's eagle">Haast's eagle</a>, which preyed upon the <i>moa</i>. Marine mammals—seals in particular—thronged the coasts, with evidence of coastal colonies much further north than those which remain today<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Huge numbers of <i>moa</i> bones—estimated to be from between 29,000 and 90,000 birds—have been located at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Waitaki_River" title="Waitaki River">Waitaki River</a>, between <a href="/wiki/Timaru" title="Timaru">Timaru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oamaru" title="Oamaru">Oamaru</a> on the east coast of the <a href="/wiki/South_Island" title="South Island">South Island</a>. Further south, at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Shag_River" title="Shag River">Shag River</a> (<i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">Waihemo</i>), evidence suggests that at least 6,000 <i>moa</i> were slaughtered by humans over a relatively short period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeology has shown that the <a href="/wiki/Otago" title="Otago">Otago</a> region was the node of Māori cultural development during this time, and the majority of archaic settlements were on or within 10 km (6 mi) of the coast. It was common for people to establish small temporary camps far inland for seasonal hunting. Settlements ranged in size from 40 people (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Palliser_Bay" title="Palliser Bay">Palliser Bay</a> in Wellington) to between 300 and 400 people, with 40 buildings (such as at the Shag River).
</p><p>The best-known and most extensively studied Archaic site is at <a href="/wiki/Wairau_Bar" title="Wairau Bar">Wairau Bar</a> in the South Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The site is similar to eastern Polynesian nucleated villages. <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon dating</a> shows the site was occupied from about 1288 to 1300. Due to tectonic forces, some of the Wairau Bar site is now underwater. Work on the Wairau Bar skeletons in 2010 showed that life expectancy was very short, the oldest skeleton being 39 and most people dying in their 20s. Most of the adults showed signs of dietary or infection stress. <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">Anemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">arthritis</a> were common. Infections such as <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> (TB) may have been present, as the symptoms were present in several skeletons. On average, the adults were taller than other South Pacific people, at 175 centimetres (5 ft 9 in) for males and 161 cm (5 ft 3 in) for females.
</p><p>The Archaic period is remarkable for the lack of weapons and fortifications so typical of the later "Classic" Māori,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moa66_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-moa66-35">[35]</a></sup> and for its distinctive "reel necklaces".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NgaKakano_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-NgaKakano-36">[36]</a></sup> From this period onward, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_New_Zealand_animals#Extinctions_since_14th_century_Māori_settlement" title="List of extinct New Zealand animals">32 species of birds became extinct</a>, either through over-predation by humans and the <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">kiore</i> and <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Kur%C4%AB" title="Kurī">kurī</a></i> (dog) they introduced;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> repeated burning of the grassland that changed their habitat, or climate cooling, which appears to have occurred from about 1400–1450. The early Māori enjoyed a rich, varied diet of birds, fish, seals and shellfish. <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">Moa</i> were also an important source of meat. According to Professor Allan Cooper, the people slaughtered to extinction most of the various lost species within 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houghton,_Philip_1980_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houghton,_Philip_1980-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Work by Helen Leach shows that Māori were using about 36 different food plants, although many required detoxification and long periods (12–24 hours) of cooking. D. Sutton's research on early Māori fertility found that first pregnancy occurred at about 20 years and the mean number of births was low, compared with other neolithic societies. The low number of births may have been due to the very low average life expectancy of 31–32 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houghton,_Philip_1980_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Houghton,_Philip_1980-38">[38]</a></sup> Analysis of skeletons at Wairau Bar showed signs of a hard life, with many having had broken bones that had healed. This suggests that the people ate a balanced diet and enjoyed a supportive community that had the resources to support severely injured family members.
</p>
<h3><span id="Classic_period_.281500.E2.80.931642.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classic_period_(1500–1642)">Classic period (1500–1642)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classic period (1500–1642)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1599" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg/250px-Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg/375px-Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg/500px-Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Model_Of_Maori_Pa_On_Headland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of a <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/P%C4%81" title="Pā">pā</a></i> (hillfort) built on a headland. <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">Pā</i> proliferated as competition and warfare increased among a growing population.</div></div></div>
<p>The cooling of the climate, confirmed by a detailed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_ring" title="Tree ring">tree-ring</a> study near <a href="/wiki/Hokitika" title="Hokitika">Hokitika</a>, shows a significant, sudden and long-lasting <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">cooler period</a> from 1500. This coincided with a series of massive earthquakes in the South Island Alpine fault, a major earthquake in 1460 in the <a href="/wiki/Wellington" title="Wellington">Wellington</a> area,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsunamis" title="Tsunamis">tsunamis</a> that destroyed many coastal settlements, and the extinction of the <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">moa</i> and other food species. These were likely factors that led to sweeping changes in the Māori culture, which developed into the most well-known "Classic" period<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> that was in place at the time of European contact.
</p><p>This period is characterised by finely made <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Pounamu" title="Pounamu">pounamu</a></i> (greenstone) weapons and ornaments, elaborately carved canoes—a tradition that was later extended to and continued in elaborately carved meeting houses called <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Wharenui" title="Wharenui">wharenui</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neich_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-neich-41">[41]</a></sup>—and a fierce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warrior_culture" title="Warrior culture">warrior culture</a>. They developed <a href="/wiki/Hillfort" title="Hillfort">hillforts</a> known as <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/P%C4%81" title="Pā">pā</a></i>, practiced <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">cannibalism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NZ_Herald_10462390_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-NZ_Herald_10462390-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and built some of the largest <a href="/wiki/Waka_(canoe)" title="Waka (canoe)">war canoes</a> ever.
</p><p>Around the year 1500, a group of Māori migrated east to the <a href="/wiki/Chatham_Islands" title="Chatham Islands">Chatham Islands</a>, where, by adapting to the local climate and the availability of resources, they developed into a people known as the <a href="/wiki/Moriori" title="Moriori">Moriori</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> related to but distinct from the Māori of mainland New Zealand. A notable feature of Moriori culture was an emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a>. When a party of invading North Taranaki Māori arrived in 1835, few of the estimated Moriori population of 2,000 survived; they were killed outright and many were enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest battle ever fought in New Zealand, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hingakaka" title="Battle of Hingakaka">Battle of Hingakaka</a>, occurred around 1780–90, south of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%C5%8Chaup%C5%8D&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ōhaupō (page does not exist)">Ōhaupō</a> on a ridge near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ngaroto" title="Lake Ngaroto">Lake Ngaroto</a>. The battle was fought between about 7,000 warriors from a <a href="/wiki/Taranaki" title="Taranaki">Taranaki</a>-led force and a much smaller <a href="/wiki/Waikato" title="Waikato">Waikato</a> force under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Te_Rauangaanga" title="Te Rauangaanga">Te Rauangaanga</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Early_European_contact_.281642.E2.80.931840.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_European_contact_(1642–1840)">Early European contact (1642–1840)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early European contact (1642–1840)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gilsemans_1642.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1948" data-file-width="3000" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gilsemans_1642.jpg/260px-Gilsemans_1642.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gilsemans_1642.jpg/390px-Gilsemans_1642.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gilsemans_1642.jpg/520px-Gilsemans_1642.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gilsemans_1642.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first European impression of Māori, at <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bay" title="Golden Bay">Murderers' Bay</a> in <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travel_journal" title="Travel journal">travel journal</a> (1642).</div></div></div>
<p>European settlement of New Zealand occurred in relatively recent historical times. New Zealand historian <a href="/wiki/Michael_King" title="Michael King">Michael King</a> in <i>The Penguin History Of New Zealand</i> describes the Māori as "the last major human community on earth untouched and unaffected by the wider world." Early European explorers, including <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a> (who arrived in 1642) and Captain <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> (who first visited in 1769), recorded their impressions of Māori. Initial contact between Māori and Europeans proved problematic and sometimes fatal, with several accounts of Europeans being cannibalised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_(1889).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="640" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg/260px-Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg/390px-Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg/520px-Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_%281889%29.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_John_Steel_-_The_blowing_up_of_the_Boyd_Steele_(1889).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Blowing Up of the Boyd</i> by <a href="/wiki/Louis_John_Steele" title="Louis John Steele">Louis John Steele</a>, 1889</div></div></div>
<p>From the 1780s, Māori encountered European and American <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">sealers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whalers</a>; some Māori crewed on the foreign ships, with many crewing on whaling and sealing ships that operated in New Zealand waters. Some of the South Island crews were almost totally Māori. Between 1800 and 1820, there were 65 sealing voyages and 106 whaling voyages to New Zealand, mainly from Britain and Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> A trickle of escaped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convictism_in_Australia" title="Convictism in Australia">convicts from Australia</a> and deserters from visiting ships, as well as early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_(Christian)" title="Mission (Christian)">Christian missionaries</a>, also exposed the indigenous population to outside influences. In the <a href="/wiki/Boyd_massacre" title="Boyd massacre">Boyd Massacre</a> in 1809, Māori took hostage and killed 66 members of the crew and passengers in apparent revenge for the captain's whipping the son of a Māori chief. Given accounts of cannibalism in this attack, shipping companies and missionaries kept their distance, significantly reducing their contact with the Māori for several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The runaways were of various standing within Māori society, ranging from slaves to high-ranking advisors. Some runaways remained little more than prisoners, while others abandoned European culture and identified as Māori. These Europeans "gone native" became known as <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81keh%C4%81_M%C4%81ori" title="Pākehā Māori">Pākehā Māori</a>. Many Māori valued them as a means to acquire European knowledge and technology, particularly firearms. When Whiria (<a href="/wiki/P%C5%8Dmare_II_(Ng%C4%81puhi)" title="Pōmare II (Ngāpuhi)">Pōmare II</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pom2_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pom2-50">[50]</a></sup> led a war-party against <a href="/wiki/T%C4%ABtore" title="Tītore">Tītore</a> in 1838, he had 131 Europeans among his warriors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Edward_Maning" title="Frederick Edward Maning">Frederick Edward Maning</a>, an early settler, wrote two lively accounts of life in these times, which have become classics of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_literature" title="New Zealand literature">New Zealand literature</a>: <i>Old New Zealand</i> and <i>History of the War in the North of New Zealand against the Chief Heke</i>. European settlement of New Zealand increased steadily. By 1839, estimates placed the number of Europeans living among the Māori as high as 2,000; two-thirds lived in the North Island, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Northland_Peninsula" title="Northland Peninsula">Northland Peninsula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contact with Europeans led to a sharing of concepts. The Māori language was first written down by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kendall" title="Thomas Kendall">Thomas Kendall</a> in 1815, in <i><a href="/wiki/A_korao_no_New_Zealand" title="A korao no New Zealand">A korao no New Zealand</a></i>; this was followed five years later by <i>A Grammar and Vocabulary of the New Zealand Language</i>, compiled by Professor <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Lee_(linguist)" title="Samuel Lee (linguist)">Samuel Lee</a> and aided by Kendall, Waikato Māori and the chief <a href="/wiki/Hongi_Hika" title="Hongi Hika">Hongi Hika</a>, on a visit to England in 1820. Māori quickly adopted writing as a means of sharing ideas, and many of their oral stories and poems were converted to the written form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>  Between February 1835 and January 1840, <a href="/wiki/William_Colenso" title="William Colenso">William Colenso</a> printed 74,000 Māori-language booklets from his press at <a href="/wiki/Pahia" title="Pahia">Pahia</a>. In 1843, the government distributed free gazettes to Māori called <i>Ko Te Karere O Nui Tireni.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> These contained information about law and crimes, with explanations and remarks about European customs, and were "designed to pass on official information to Māori and to encourage the idea that Pākehā and Māori were contracted together under the Treaty of Waitangi".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1805 and 1840, the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">muskets</a> by tribes in close contact with European visitors upset the balance of power among Māori tribes. This led to a period of bloody <a href="/wiki/Endemic_warfare" title="Endemic warfare">intertribal warfare</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Musket_Wars" title="Musket Wars">Musket Wars</a>, which resulted in the decimation of several tribes and the driving of others from their traditional territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> It has been estimated that during this period the Māori population dropped from about 100,000 (in 1800) to between 50,000 and 80,000 by the wars' end in 1843. The 1850s were a decade of relative stability and economic growth for Māori.
</p><p>The picture is confused by uncertainty over how or if Pākehā Māori were counted, and the severe dislocation of many of the less powerful <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">iwi</i> and <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Hap%C5%AB" title="Hapū">hapū</a></i> (subtribes) during the wars. The smashing of normal society by the four decades of wars and the driving of peaceful tribes from their productive turangawaewae, such as the Moriori in the <a href="/wiki/Chatham_Islands" title="Chatham Islands">Chatham Islands</a> by invading forces from North Taranaki, had a catastrophic effect on these conquered tribes.
</p><p>At the same time, the Māori suffered high mortality rates for new Eurasian infectious diseases, such as <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, which killed an unknown number of Māori: estimates vary between ten and fifty percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The spread of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidemics" title="Epidemics">epidemics</a> resulted largely from the Māori lacking acquired <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> to the new diseases. A huge influx of European settlers in the 1870s increased contact among many of the indigenous people with the newcomers.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Te_Rangi_H%C4%ABroa" title="Te Rangi Hīroa">Te Rangi Hīroa</a> documents an epidemic caused by a respiratory disease that Māori called <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">rewharewha</i>. It "decimated" populations in the early 19th century and "spread with extraordinary virulence throughout the North Island and even to the South... Measles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scarlet_fever" title="Scarlet fever">scarlet fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whooping_cough" title="Whooping cough">whooping cough</a> and almost everything, except <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_trypanosomiasis" title="African trypanosomiasis">sleeping sickness</a>, have taken their toll of Māori dead."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Economic changes also took a toll: migration of Māori workers into unhealthy swamplands to produce and export <a href="/wiki/Phormium" title="Phormium">flax</a> led to further mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Treaty_with_the_British_Crown_.281840.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Treaty_with_the_British_Crown_(1840)">Treaty with the British Crown (1840)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Treaty with the British Crown (1840)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:136px;max-width:136px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:218px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HoneHeke1845.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="356" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/HoneHeke1845.jpg/134px-HoneHeke1845.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/HoneHeke1845.jpg/201px-HoneHeke1845.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/HoneHeke1845.jpg/268px-HoneHeke1845.jpg 2x" width="134"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">One of the signatories of the treaty, <a href="/wiki/H%C5%8Dne_Heke" title="Hōne Heke">Hōne Heke</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81puhi" title="Ngāpuhi">Ngāpuhi</a> iwi, with his wife Hariata</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:218px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1319" data-file-width="903" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg/150px-TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg/225px-TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg/300px-TamatiWakaNene1870s.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/T%C4%81mati_W%C4%81ka_Nene" title="Tāmati Wāka Nene">Tāmati Wāka Nene</a> of Ngāpuhi was a signatory of the treaty, and was influential in convincing others to sign.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>With increasing Christian <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> activity and growing European settlement in the 1830s, and with growing lawlessness in New Zealand, the <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">British Crown</a> acceded to repeated requests from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missionaries" title="Missionaries">missionaries</a> and some chiefs (<i><a href="/wiki/Rangatira" title="Rangatira">rangatira</a></i>) to intervene. Some freewheeling escaped convicts and seamen, as well as gunrunners and Americans actively worked against the British government by spreading rumours amongst the Māori that the government would oppress and mistreat them. Tamati Waka Nene, a pro-government chief, was angry that the government had not taken active steps to stop gunrunners selling weapons to rebels in Hokianga. In addition, France appeared to be showing interest in acquiring New Zealand to add to its stake in Polynesia. British immigrants believed that the French Catholic missionaries were spreading anti-British feeling. All of the chiefs who spoke against the Treaty on 5 February 1840 were Catholic. Years after the treaty was signed, Bishop <a href="/wiki/Jean_Baptiste_Pompallier" title="Jean Baptiste Pompallier">Pompallier</a> admitted that all the Catholic chiefs and especially Rewa, had consulted him for advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ultimately, the British government sent Royal Navy Captain <a href="/wiki/William_Hobson" title="William Hobson">William Hobson</a> with instructions to negotiate a treaty between the British Crown and the people of New Zealand. Soon after arrival in New Zealand in February 1840, Hobson negotiated a treaty with North Island chiefs, later to become known as the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi" title="Treaty of Waitangi">Treaty of Waitangi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In the end, 500 tribal chiefs and a small number of Europeans signed the Treaty, while some chiefs — such as <a href="/wiki/P%C5%8Dtatau_Te_Wherowhero" title="Pōtatau Te Wherowhero">Te Wherowhero</a> in Waikato<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> — refused to sign. The Treaty gave Māori the rights of <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British subjects</a> and guaranteed Māori property rights and tribal autonomy, in return for accepting British <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Considerable dispute continues over aspects of the Treaty of Waitangi. The original treaty was written mainly by Busby and translated into Māori by Henry Williams, who was moderately proficient in Māori, and his son William, who was more skilled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> They were handicapped by their imperfect Māori and the lack of exactly similar words in Māori, as well as by deep differences among the peoples on concepts of property rights and sovereignty, for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> At Waitangi the chiefs signed the Māori translation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_disputes_and_conflict">Land disputes and conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Land disputes and conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Wars" title="New Zealand Wars">New Zealand Wars</a></div>
<p>Despite conflicting interpretations of the provisions of the Treaty of Waitangi, relations between Māori and Europeans during the early colonial period were largely peaceful. Many Māori groups set up substantial businesses, supplying food and other products for domestic and overseas markets. Among the early European settlers who learnt the Māori language and recorded <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_mythology" title="Māori mythology">Māori mythology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Edward_Grey" title="George Edward Grey">George Grey</a>, Governor of New Zealand from 1845–1855 and 1861–1868, stands out.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto,_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1629" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg/170px-Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg/255px-Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg/340px-Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto%2C_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rewi_Manga_Maniapoto,_by_Gottfried_Lindauer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A portrait of Māori man, by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Lindauer" title="Gottfried Lindauer">Gottfried Lindauer</a>, 1882</div></div></div>
<p>However, rising tensions over disputed land purchases and attempts by Māori in the <a href="/wiki/Waikato" title="Waikato">Waikato</a> to establish what some saw as a rival to the British system of royalty led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_wars" title="New Zealand wars">New Zealand wars</a> in the 1860s. These conflicts started when rebel Māori attacked isolated settlers in Taranaki but were fought mainly between Crown troops—from both Britain and new regiments raised in Australia, aided by settlers and some allied Māori (known as <i>kupapa</i>)—and numerous Māori groups opposed to the disputed land sales, including some Waikato Māori.
</p><p>While these conflicts resulted in few Māori (compared to the earlier Musket wars) or European deaths, the colonial government <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_land_confiscations" title="New Zealand land confiscations">confiscated</a> tracts of tribal land as punishment for what were called rebellions. In some cases the government confiscated land from tribes that had taken no part in the war, although this was almost immediately returned. Some of the confiscated land was returned to both kupapa and "rebel" Māori. Several minor conflicts also arose after the wars, including the incident at <a href="/wiki/Parihaka" title="Parihaka">Parihaka</a> in 1881 and the <a href="/wiki/Dog_Tax_War" title="Dog Tax War">Dog Tax War</a> from 1897–98.
</p><p>The Native Land Acts of 1862 and 1865 established the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_Land_Court" title="Native Land Court">Native Land Court</a>, which was intended to transfer Māori land from communal ownership into individual household title as a means to assimilation and to facilitate greater sales to European immigrants. Māori land under individual title became available to be sold to the colonial government or to settlers in private sales. Between 1840 and 1890, Māori sold 95 percent of their land (63,000,000 of 66,000,000 acres (270,000 km<sup>2</sup>) in 1890). In total 4 per cent of this was confiscated land, although about a quarter of this was returned. 300,000 acres was returned to Kupapa Māori mainly in the lower Waikato River Basin area. Individual Māori titleholders received considerable capital from these land sales, with some lower Waikato Chiefs being given 1000 pounds each. Disputes later arose over whether or not promised compensation in some sales was fully delivered. Some claim that later, the selling off of Māori land and the lack of appropriate skills hampered Māori participation in developing the New Zealand economy, eventually diminishing the capacity of many Māori to sustain themselves.
</p><p>The Māori MP <a href="/wiki/Henare_Kaihau" title="Henare Kaihau">Henare Kaihau</a>, from Waiuku, who was executive head of the King Movement, worked alongside King <a href="/wiki/Mahuta_T%C4%81whiao" title="Mahuta Tāwhiao">Mahuta</a> to sell land to the government. At that time the king sold 185,000 acres per year. In 1910 the Māori Land Conference at Waihi discussed selling a further 600,000 acres. King Mahuta had been successful in getting restitution for some blocks of land previously confiscated, and these were returned to the King in his name. Henare Kaihau invested all the money- 50,000 pounds- in an Auckland land company which collapsed; all 50,000 pounds of the kingitanga money was lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TawhiaoNLA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="407" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/TawhiaoNLA.jpg/170px-TawhiaoNLA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/TawhiaoNLA.jpg/255px-TawhiaoNLA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/TawhiaoNLA.jpg/340px-TawhiaoNLA.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TawhiaoNLA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/T%C4%81whiao" title="Tāwhiao">Tāwhiao</a>, the second Māori King</div></div></div>
<p>In 1884 King <a href="/wiki/T%C4%81whiao" title="Tāwhiao">Tāwhiao</a> withdrew money from the kingitanga bank, Te Peeke o Aotearoa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> to travel to London to see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen Victoria</a> to try and persuade her to honour the Treaty between their peoples. He did not get past the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">Secretary of State for the Colonies</a>, who said it was a New Zealand problem. Returning to New Zealand, the Premier <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stout" title="Robert Stout">Robert Stout</a> insisted that all events happening before 1863 were the responsibility of the Imperial Government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1891 Māori comprised just 10 per cent of the population but still owned 17 per cent of the land, although much of it was of poor quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_revival">Decline and revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Decline and revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="560" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg/200px-Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg/300px-Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg/400px-Bella_-_Maori_woman_with_husband_and_child_and_2_dogs_in_front_of_home_at_Hot_Springs_LCCN2004707870.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Bella, a Māori woman with her husband, child and two dogs in front of their home at <a href="/wiki/Whakarewarewa" title="Whakarewarewa">Whakarewarewa</a>, 1895</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="514" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg/200px-Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg/300px-Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg/400px-Maori_woman_and_children_playing_cards_on_doorstep_of_home_-_Whakarewarewa_LCCN2004707876.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Māori woman and children playing cards on doorstep of their home, <a href="/wiki/Whakarewarewa" title="Whakarewarewa">Whakarewarewa</a>, 1895</div></div></div></div>
<p>By the late 19th century a widespread belief existed amongst both Pākehā and Māori that the Māori population would cease to exist as a separate race or culture, and become assimilated into the European population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In 1840, New Zealand had a Māori population of about 50,000 to 70,000 and only about 2,000 Europeans. By 1860 the Europeans had increased to 50,000. The Māori population had declined to 37,520 in the 1871 census, although <a href="/wiki/Te_Rangi_H%C4%ABroa" title="Te Rangi Hīroa">Te Rangi Hīroa</a> (Sir Peter Buck) believed this figure was too low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The figure was 42,113 in the 1896 census, by which time Europeans numbered more than 700,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Professor Ian Pool noticed that as late as 1890, 40 per cent of all female Māori children who were born died before the age of one, a much higher rate than for males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The decline of the Māori population did not continue; it stabilized and began to recover. By 1936 the Māori figure was 82,326, although the sudden rise in the 1930s was probably due to the introduction of the family benefit − payable only when a birth was registered, according to Professor Pool. Despite a substantial level of <a href="/wiki/Interracial_marriage" title="Interracial marriage">intermarriage</a> between the Māori and European populations, many ethnic Māori retained their cultural identity. A number of discourses developed as to the meaning of "Māori" and to who counted as Māori or not.
</p><p>The parliament instituted four <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_electorates" title="Māori electorates">Māori seats</a> in 1867, giving all Māori men universal suffrage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElectionsNZ_Maori_vote_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElectionsNZ_Maori_vote-76">[76]</a></sup> 12 years ahead of their European New Zealand counterparts. Until the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_1879" title="New Zealand general election, 1879">1879 general elections</a>, men had to satisfy property requirements of landowning or rental payments to qualify as voters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> New Zealand was thus the first neo-European nation in the world to give the vote to its indigenous people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> While the Māori seats encouraged Māori participation in politics, the relative size of the Māori population of the time <i>vis à vis</i> <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81keh%C4%81" title="Pākehā">Pākehā</a> would have warranted approximately 15 seats. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ApiranaNgata05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="402" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/ApiranaNgata05.jpg/200px-ApiranaNgata05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/ApiranaNgata05.jpg/300px-ApiranaNgata05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/ApiranaNgata05.jpg/400px-ApiranaNgata05.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ApiranaNgata05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir <a href="/wiki/%C4%80pirana_Ngata" title="Āpirana Ngata">Āpirana Ngata</a> became instrumental in the revival of traditional arts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kapa_haka" title="Kapa haka">kapa haka</a></i> and carving.</div></div></div>
<p>From the late 19th century, successful Māori politicians such as <a href="/wiki/James_Carroll_(New_Zealand_politician)" title="James Carroll (New Zealand politician)">James Carroll</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C4%80pirana_Ngata" title="Āpirana Ngata">Āpirana Ngata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Te_Rangi_H%C4%ABroa" title="Te Rangi Hīroa">Te Rangi Hīroa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maui_Pomare" title="Maui Pomare">Maui Pomare</a>, were influential in politics. At one point Carroll became Acting <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_New_Zealand" title="Prime Minister of New Zealand">Prime Minister</a>. The group, known as the <a href="/wiki/Young_M%C4%81ori_Party" title="Young Māori Party">Young Māori Party</a>, cut across voting-blocs in Parliament and aimed to revitalise the Māori people after the devastation of the previous century. They believed the future path called for a degree of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> with Māori adopting European practices such as <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine#Modern_medicine" title="History of medicine">Western medicine</a> and education, especially learning English.
</p><p>Ngata acted as a major force behind the revival of arts such as <a href="/wiki/Kapa_haka" title="Kapa haka">kapa haka</a> and carving. He also enacted a programme of land development, which helped many iwi retain and develop their land. Ngata became very close to <a href="/wiki/Te_Puea_Herangi" title="Te Puea Herangi">Te Puea</a>, the Waikato kingite leader, who was supported by the government in her attempt to improve living conditions for Waikato. Ngata transferred four blocks of land to Te Puea and her husband and arranged extensive government grants and loans. Ngata sacked the pakeha farm development officer and replaced him with Te Puea. He arranged for her to have a car to travel around the various farms. Te Puea's husband was also given a large farm at Tikitere near Rotorua. The public, media and parliament became alarmed at the flow of funds from government to Te Puea during the recession. A Royal Commission was held in 1934 that found Ngata guilty of maladministration and misappropriation of funds to the value of 500,000 pounds. Ngata was forced to resign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>, a Māori pioneer force was taken to Egypt but quickly was turned into a successful combat infantry battalion; in the last years of the war it was known as the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Battalion" title="Māori Battalion">Māori Battalion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> It mainly comprised Te Arawa, Te Aitanga-a-Māhaki, Te Aitanga-a-Hauiti, Ngāti Porou and Ngāti Kahungun and later many Cook Islanders; the Waikato and Taranaki tribes refused to enlist or be <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscripted</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Māori were badly hit by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1918_influenza_epidemic" title="1918 influenza epidemic">1918 influenza epidemic</a> when the Māori battalion returned from the Western Front. The death rate from influenza for Māori was 4.5 times higher than for Pakeha. Many Māori, especially in the Waikato, were very reluctant to visit a doctor and went to a hospital only when the patient was nearly dead. To cope with isolation, Waikato Māori, under Te Puea's leadership, increasingly returned to the old <a href="/wiki/Pai_M%C4%81rire" title="Pai Mārire">Pai Mārire</a> (Hau hau) cult of the 1860s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until 1893, 53 years after the Treaty of Waitangi, Māori did not pay tax on land holdings. In 1893 a very light tax was payable only on leasehold land, and it was not till 1917 that Māori were required to pay a heavier tax equal to half that paid by other New Zealanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, the government decided to exempt Māori from the conscription that applied to other citizens. The Māori volunteered in large numbers, forming the 28th or <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Battalion" title="Māori Battalion">Māori Battalion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Response_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Response-85">[85]</a></sup> which performed creditably, notably in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Altogether 16,000 Māori took part in the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW2_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-WW2-87">[87]</a></sup> Māori, including Cook Islanders, made up 12 per cent of the total New Zealand force. 3,600 served in the Māori Battalion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Response_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Response-85">[85]</a></sup> the remainder serving in artillery, pioneers, home guard, infantry, airforce, and navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW2_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-WW2-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="427" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg/180px-Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg/270px-Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg/360px-Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whina_Cooper_in_Hamilton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Whina_Cooper" title="Whina Cooper">Whina Cooper</a> leads the Māori Land March through Hamilton in 1975, seeking redress for historical grievances.</div></div></div>
<p>Many Māori migrated to larger rural towns and cities during the Depression and post-WWII periods in search of employment, leaving rural communities depleted and disconnecting many urban Māori from their traditional ways of life. Yet while standards of living improved among Māori during this time, they continued to lag behind Pākehā in areas such as health, income, skilled employment and access to higher levels of education. Māori leaders and government policymakers alike struggled to deal with social issues stemming from increased urban migration, including a shortage of housing and jobs, and a rise in urban crime, poverty and health problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorrenson_1997,_pp_339–41_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorrenson_1997,_pp_339–41-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Recent_history_.281960s.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history_(1960s–present)">Recent history (1960s–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Recent history (1960s–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the 1960s, Māoridom has undergone a cultural revival<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> concurrent with activism for social justice and a <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_protest_movement" title="Māori protest movement">protest movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
Government recognition of the growing political power of Māori and political activism have led to limited <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi_claims_and_settlements" title="Treaty of Waitangi claims and settlements">redress</a> for confiscation of land and for the violation of other <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property rights</a>. In 1975 the Crown set up the <a href="/wiki/Waitangi_Tribunal" title="Waitangi Tribunal">Waitangi Tribunal</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> a body with the powers of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commission_of_Enquiry" title="Commission of Enquiry">Commission of Enquiry</a>, to investigate and make recommendations on such issues, but it cannot make binding rulings; the Government need not accept the findings of the Waitangi Tribunal, and has rejected some of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Since 1976, people of Māori descent may choose to enroll on either the general or Māori roll, and vote in either the Māori only or general electorates, but not both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seats_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-seats-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1990s and 2000s, the government negotiated with Māori to provide redress for breaches by the Crown of the guarantees set out in the Treaty of Waitangi in 1840. By 2006 the government had provided over NZ$900 million in settlements, much of it in the form of land deals. The largest settlement, signed on 25 June 2008 with seven Māori iwi, transferred nine large tracts of forested land to Māori control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> As a result of the redress paid to many iwi, Māori now have significant interests in the fishing and forestry industries. There is a growing Māori leadership who are using the treaty settlements as an investment platform for economic development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeraldJames_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-HeraldJames-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite a growing acceptance of Māori culture in wider New Zealand society, the settlements have generated controversy on both sides. Some Māori have complained that the settlements occur at a level of between 1 and 2.5 cents on the dollar of the value of the confiscated lands; conversely, some non-Māori denounce the settlements and socioeconomic iniatives as amounting to race-based preferential treatment. Both of these sentiments were expressed during the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_foreshore_and_seabed_controversy" title="New Zealand foreshore and seabed controversy">New Zealand foreshore and seabed controversy</a> in 2004.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/2013_New_Zealand_census" title="2013 New Zealand census">2013 census</a>, 598,605 people identified as being part of the Māori ethnic group, accounting for 14.9 per cent of the New Zealand population, while 668,724 people (17.5 per cent) claimed Māori descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quickstats_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quickstats-96">[96]</a></sup> Of those identifying as Māori, 278,199 people identified as of sole Māori ethnicity while 260,229 identified as of both European and Māori ethnicity, due to the high rate of intermarriage between the two cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Under the Maori Affairs Amendment Act 1974, a Māori is defined as "a person of the Māori race of New Zealand; and includes any descendant of such a person".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2013 census, the largest iwi by population is <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81puhi" title="Ngāpuhi">Ngāpuhi</a> (125,601), followed by <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81ti_Porou" title="Ngāti Porou">Ngāti Porou</a> (71,049), <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81i_Tahu" title="Ngāi Tahu">Ngāi Tahu</a> (54,819) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waikato_(iwi)" title="Waikato (iwi)">Waikato</a> (40,083). However, over 110,000 people of Māori descent could not identify their iwi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quickstats_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quickstats-96">[96]</a></sup> Outside of New Zealand, a large Māori population exists in Australia, estimated at 155,000 in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamer_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamer-99">[99]</a></sup> The Māori Party has suggested a special seat should be created in the New Zealand parliament representing Māori in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Smaller communities also exist in the United Kingdom (approx. 8,000), the United States (up to 3,500) and Canada (approx. 1,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walrond_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Walrond-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uscb_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-uscb-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statcan_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-statcan-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_culture" title="Māori culture">Māori culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KupeWheke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1570" data-file-width="389" height="484" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/KupeWheke.jpg/120px-KupeWheke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/KupeWheke.jpg/180px-KupeWheke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/KupeWheke.jpg/240px-KupeWheke.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KupeWheke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Late 20th-century <a href="/wiki/Whakairo" title="Whakairo">carving</a> depicting the mythological navigator <a href="/wiki/Kupe" title="Kupe">Kupe</a> fighting two sea creatures.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_culture">Traditional culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Traditional culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaoriChief1784.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2635" data-file-width="1995" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/MaoriChief1784.jpg/180px-MaoriChief1784.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/MaoriChief1784.jpg/270px-MaoriChief1784.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/MaoriChief1784.jpg/360px-MaoriChief1784.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MaoriChief1784.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Māori chief with tattoos (<a href="/wiki/T%C4%81_moko" title="Tā moko">moko</a>) seen by James Cook and his crew.</div></div></div>
<p>The ancestors of the Māori arrived from eastern Polynesia during the 13th century, bringing with them Polynesian cultural customs and beliefs. Early European researchers, such as <a href="/wiki/Julius_von_Haast" title="Julius von Haast">Julius von Haast</a>, a geologist, incorrectly interpreted archaeological remains as belonging to a pre-Māori <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> people; later researchers, notably <a href="/wiki/Percy_Smith_(ethnologist)" title="Percy Smith (ethnologist)">Percy Smith</a>, magnified such theories into an elaborate scenario with a series of sharply-defined cultural stages which had Māori arriving in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Fleet" title="Great Fleet">Great Fleet</a> in 1350 and replacing the so-called "moa-hunter" culture with a "classical Māori" culture based on horticulture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The development of Māori <a href="/wiki/Material_culture" title="Material culture">material culture</a> has been similarly delineated by the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_New_Zealand_Te_Papa_Tongarewa" title="Museum of New Zealand Te Papa Tongarewa">Museum of New Zealand Te Papa Tongarewa</a> into "cultural periods", from the earlier "Ngā Kakano" stage to the later "Te Tipunga" period, before the "Classic" period of Māori history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NgaKakano_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-NgaKakano-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_Noteiv"><a href="#endnote_Noteiv">[iv]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tipunga_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-tipunga-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the archaeological record indicates a gradual evolution of a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">neolithic</a> culture that varied in pace and extent according to local resources and conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In the course of a few centuries, the growing population led to competition for resources and an increase in warfare. The archaeological record reveals an increased frequency of fortified <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81" title="Pā">pā</a>, although debate continues about the amount of conflict.
