Various systems arose which aimed to conserve resources; most of these, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tapu_(Polynesian_culture)" title="Tapu (Polynesian culture)">tapu</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rahui" title="Rahui">rāhui</a></i>, used religious or supernatural threats to discourage people from taking species at particular seasons or from specified areas.
</p><p>Warfare between tribes was common, generally over land conflicts or to restore <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text"><a href="/wiki/Mana" title="Mana">mana</a></i>. Fighting was carried out between <i lang="mi" title="Māori language text">hapū</i>. Although not practised during times of peace, Māori would sometimes eat their conquered enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> As Māori continued in geographic isolation, performing arts such as the <a href="/wiki/Haka" title="Haka">haka</a> developed from their Polynesian roots, as did carving and weaving. Regional dialects arose, with differences in vocabulary and in the pronunciation of some words. In 1819 two young northern chiefs, Tuai and Titere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> who had learnt to speak and write English, went to London, where they met the language expert Samuel Lee. They stayed with a school teacher, Hall, who they told that even in Northern New Zealand there were "different languages and dialects".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The language retained enough similarities to other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Polynesian_languages" title="Eastern Polynesian languages">Eastern Polynesian languages</a>, to the point where <a href="/wiki/Tupaia_(navigator)" title="Tupaia (navigator)">Tupaia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tahitians" title="Tahitians">Tahitian</a> navigator on <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a>'s first voyage in the region acted as an interpreter between Māori and the crew of the <i><a href="/wiki/HMS_Endeavour" title="HMS Endeavour">Endeavour</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief_and_religion">Belief and religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Belief and religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_mythology" title="Māori mythology">Māori mythology</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_religion" title="Māori religion">Māori religion</a></div>
<p>Traditional Māori beliefs have their origins in Polynesian culture. Many stories from Māori mythology are analogous with stories across the Pacific Ocean. Polynesian concepts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tapu_(Polynesian_culture)" title="Tapu (Polynesian culture)">tapu</a></i> (sacred), <i><a href="/wiki/Tapu_(Polynesian_culture)#Noa" title="Tapu (Polynesian culture)">noa</a></i> (non-sacred), <i><a href="/wiki/Mana" title="Mana">mana</a></i> (authority/prestige) and <i>wairua</i> (spirit) governed everyday Māori living. These practices remained until the arrival of Europeans, when much of Māori religion and mythology was supplanted by Christianity. Today, Māori "tend to be followers of <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> (Mormons), or Māori Christian groups such as <a href="/wiki/R%C4%81tana" title="Rātana">Rātana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ringat%C5%AB" title="Ringatū">Ringatū</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion-108">[108]</a></sup> but with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> groupings also prominent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_New_Zealand" title="Islam in New Zealand">Islam</a> is estimated as the fastest growing religion among Māori,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> yet <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Muslims" title="Māori Muslims">Māori Muslims</a> constitute a very small proportion of Māori.
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/2013_New_Zealand_census" title="2013 New Zealand census">2013 New Zealand census</a>, 8.8 percent of Māori were affiliated with Māori Christian denominations and 39.6 percent with other Christian denominations; 46.3 percent of Māori claimed <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_in_New_Zealand" title="Irreligion in New Zealand">no religion</a>. Proportions of Christian and irreligious Māori are comparable with European New Zealanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="2006" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg/180px-Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg/270px-Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg/360px-Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Maori_man_dancing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young man performs in a <a href="/wiki/Kapa_haka" title="Kapa haka">kapa haka</a> group at a <a href="/wiki/Rotorua" title="Rotorua">Rotorua</a> tourist venue.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performing_arts">Performing arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Performing arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Kapa_haka" title="Kapa haka">Kapa haka</a></i> (literally "haka team") is a traditional Māori performance art, encompassing many forms, that is still popular today. It includes <i><a href="/wiki/Haka" title="Haka">haka</a></i> (posture dance), <a href="/wiki/Poi_(performance_art)" title="Poi (performance art)"><i>poi</i></a> (dance accompanied by song and rhythmic movements of the poi, a light ball on a string), <i>waiata-ā-ringa</i> (action songs) and <i>waiata koroua</i> (traditional chants). From the early 20th century kapa haka concert parties began touring overseas.
</p><p>Since 1972 there has been a regular competition, the <a href="/wiki/Te_Matatini" title="Te Matatini">Te Matatini</a> National Festival, organised by the Aotearoa Traditional Māori Performing Arts Society. Māori from different regions send representative groups to compete in the biennial competition. There are also kapa haka groups in schools, tertiary institutions and workplaces. It is also performed at tourist venues across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diamond_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diamond-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swarbrick_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swarbrick-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_media">Literature and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Literature and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_art#Prehistoric_art" title="New Zealand art">New Zealand art § Prehistoric art</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_art#Traditional_Māori_art" title="New Zealand art">New Zealand art § Traditional Māori art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dendroglyph" title="Dendroglyph">Dendroglyph</a></div>
<p>Like other cultures, oral folklore was used by Māori to preserve their stories and beliefs across many centuries. In the 19th century, European-style literacy was brought to the Māori, which led to Māori history documentation in books, novels and later television. Māori language use began to decline in the 20th century with English as the language through which Māori literature became widespread.
</p><p>Notable Māori novelists include <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Grace" title="Patricia Grace">Patricia Grace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Witi_Ihimaera" title="Witi Ihimaera">Witi Ihimaera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Duff" title="Alan Duff">Alan Duff</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Once_Were_Warriors_(film)" title="Once Were Warriors (film)">Once Were Warriors</a></i>, a 1994 film adapted from a 1990 <a href="/wiki/Once_Were_Warriors" title="Once Were Warriors">novel of the same name</a> by Alan Duff, brought the plight of some urban Māori to a wide audience. It was the highest-grossing film in New Zealand until 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and received international acclaim, winning several international film prizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> While some Māori feared that viewers would consider the violent male characters an accurate portrayal of Māori men, most critics praised it as exposing the raw side of <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Some Māori opinion, particularly feminist, welcomed the debate on domestic violence that the film enabled.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Well-known Māori actors and actresses include <a href="/wiki/Temuera_Morrison" title="Temuera Morrison">Temuera Morrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Curtis" title="Cliff Curtis">Cliff Curtis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Makoare" title="Lawrence Makoare">Lawrence Makoare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manu_Bennett" title="Manu Bennett">Manu Bennett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keisha_Castle-Hughes" title="Keisha Castle-Hughes">Keisha Castle-Hughes</a>. They are in films like <i><a href="/wiki/Whale_Rider" title="Whale Rider">Whale Rider</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars:_Episode_III_%E2%80%93_Revenge_of_the_Sith" title="Star Wars: Episode III – Revenge of the Sith">Star Wars: Episode III – Revenge of the Sith</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Matrix" title="The Matrix">The Matrix</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/King_Kong" title="King Kong">King Kong</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_River_Queen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The River Queen (page does not exist)">The River Queen</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings_(film_series)" title="The Lord of the Rings (film series)">The Lord of The Rings</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_(film)" title="Rapa Nui (film)">Rapa Nui</a></i>, and others, and famous television series like <i><a href="/wiki/Xena:_Warrior_Princess" title="Xena: Warrior Princess">Xena: Warrior Princess</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hercules:_The_Legendary_Journeys" title="Hercules: The Legendary Journeys">Hercules: The Legendary Journeys</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle%27s_The_Lost_World" title="Sir Arthur Conan Doyle's The Lost World">The Lost World</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Spartacus:_Blood_and_Sand" title="Spartacus: Blood and Sand">Spartacus: Blood and Sand</a></i>. In most cases their roles in <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">Hollywood</a> productions have them portraying ethnic groups other than Māori.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haka_2006.jpg"><img alt="Rugby team wearing all black, facing the camera, knees bent, and facing toward a team wearing white" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haka_2006.jpg/220px-Haka_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haka_2006.jpg/330px-Haka_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haka_2006.jpg/440px-Haka_2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haka_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Haka_(sports)" title="Haka (sports)">haka</a> performed by the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_national_rugby_union_team" title="New Zealand national rugby union team">national rugby union team</a> before a game.</div></div></div>
<p>Māori participate fully in <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_New_Zealand" title="Sport in New Zealand">New Zealand's sporting culture</a>, and are well-represented in rugby union, rugby league and netball teams at all levels. The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_national_rugby_union_team" title="New Zealand national rugby union team">New Zealand national rugby union team</a> performs a <a href="/wiki/Haka_(sports)" title="Haka (sports)">haka</a>, a traditional Māori challenge, before international matches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> As well as participation in national sports teams, there are Māori <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_All_Blacks" title="Māori All Blacks">rugby union</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_M%C4%81ori_rugby_league_team" title="New Zealand Māori rugby league team">rugby league</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_M%C4%81ori_cricket_team" title="New Zealand Māori cricket team">cricket</a> representative teams that play in international competitions.
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics" title="2016 Summer Olympics">2016 Summer Olympics</a> in Rio de Janeiro, 41 of the 199 competitors (20.5 percent) were of Māori descent in the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_at_the_2016_Summer_Olympics" title="New Zealand at the 2016 Summer Olympics">New Zealand delegation</a>, with the rugby sevens squads alone having 17 Māori competitors (out of 24). There were also three competitors of Māori descent in the <a href="/wiki/Australia_at_the_2016_Summer_Olympics" title="Australia at the 2016 Summer Olympics">Australian delegation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ki-o-rahi" title="Ki-o-rahi">Ki-o-rahi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tapawai" title="Tapawai">tapawai</a> are two sports of Māori origin. Ki-o-rahi got an unexpected boost when <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a> chose it to represent New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outrigger_canoeing" title="Outrigger canoeing">Waka ama</a> (outrigger canoeing) is also popular with Māori.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="Māori language">Māori language</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language_revival" title="Māori language revival">Māori language revival</a></div>
<p>From about 1890, Māori <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Members_of_Parliament" title="Members of Parliament">Members of Parliament</a> realised the importance of English literacy to Māori and insisted that all Māori children be taught in English. Missionaries, who still ran many Māori schools, had been teaching exclusively in Māori but the Māori MPs insisted this should stop. However attendance at school for many Māori was intermittent. 
</p><p>The Māori language, also known as <i>te reo Māori</i> (pronounced [ˈmaːoɾi, te ˈɾeo ˈmaːoɾi]) or simply <i>Te Reo</i> ("the language"), has the status of an official language. Linguists classify it within the Eastern Polynesian languages as being closely related to <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islands_M%C4%81ori" title="Cook Islands Māori">Cook Islands Māori</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuamotuan_language" title="Tuamotuan language">Tuamotuan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tahitian_language" title="Tahitian language">Tahitian</a>. Before European contact Māori did not have a written language and "important information such as whakapapa was memorised and passed down verbally through the generations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Māori were familiar with the concept of maps and when interacting with missionaries in 1815 could draw accurate maps of their <a href="/wiki/Rohe" title="Rohe">rohe</a> (iwi boundaries), onto paper, that were the equal of European maps. Missionaries surmised that Māori had traditionally drawn maps on sand or other natural materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many areas of New Zealand, Māori lost its role as a living community language used by significant numbers of people in the <a href="/wiki/Post-war" title="Post-war">post-war</a> years. In tandem with calls for sovereignty and for the righting of social injustices from the 1970s onwards, New Zealand schools now teach Māori culture and language as an option, and pre-school <i>kohanga reo</i> ("language-nests") have started, which teach <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamariki" title="Tamariki">tamariki</a></i> (young children) exclusively in Māori. These now<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> extend right through secondary schools (<i>kura tuarua</i>). Most preschool centres teach basics such as colours, numerals and greetings in Māori songs and chants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Television" title="Māori Television">Māori Television</a>, a government-funded channel committed to broadcasting primarily in Te Reo, began in March 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The 1996 census reported 160,000 Māori speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quickstats_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-quickstats-126">[126]</a></sup> At the time of the 2013 census 125,352 Māori (21.3 per cent) reported a conversational level of <a href="/wiki/Language_proficiency" title="Language proficiency">proficiency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_development">Historical development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Historical development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Polynesian settlers in New Zealand developed a distinct society over several hundred years. Social groups were tribal, with no unified society or single Māori identity until after the arrival of Europeans. Nevertheless, common elements could be found in all Māori groups in pre-European New Zealand, including a shared Polynesian heritage, a common basic language, familial associations, traditions of warfare, and similar mythologies and religious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Māori lived in villages, which were inhabited by several <i><a href="/wiki/Wh%C4%81nau" title="Whānau">whānau</a></i> (extended families) who collectively formed a <i><a href="/wiki/Hap%C5%AB" title="Hapū">hapū</a></i> (clan or subtribe). Members of a hapū cooperated with food production, gathering resources, raising families and defence. Māori society across New Zealand was broadly stratified into three classes of people: <i><a href="/wiki/Rangatira" title="Rangatira">rangatira</a></i>, chiefs and ruling families; <i>tūtūā</i>, commoners; and <i>mōkai</i>, slaves. <i><a href="/wiki/Tohunga" title="Tohunga">Tohunga</a></i> also held special standing in their communities as specialists of revered arts, skills and esoteric knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King-p42p43_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-p42p43-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAra-Taonui_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-TeAra-Taonui-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shared ancestry, intermarriage and trade strengthened relationships between different groups. Many hapū with mutually-recognised shared ancestry formed <i><a href="/wiki/Iwi" title="Iwi">iwi</a></i>, or tribes, which were the largest social unit in Māori society. Hapū and iwi often united for expeditions to gather food and resources, or in times of conflict. In contrast, warfare developed as an integral part of traditional life, as different groups competed for food and resources, settled personal disputes, and sought to increase their prestige and authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King-p42p43_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-King-p42p43-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg"><img alt="Māori whānau from Rotorua in the 1880s" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="657" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg/250px-Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg/375px-Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg/500px-Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Famille_Maori_1998-1361-139.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Māori whānau from <a href="/wiki/Rotorua" title="Rotorua">Rotorua</a> in the 1880s. Many aspects of Western life and culture, including European clothing and architecture, became incorporated into Māori society during the 19th century.</div></div></div>
<p>The arrival of Europeans to New Zealand dates back to the 17th century, although it was not until the expeditions of James Cook over a hundred years later that any meaningful interactions occurred between Europeans and Māori. For Māori, the new arrivals brought opportunities for trade, which many groups embraced eagerly. Early European settlers introduced tools, weapons, clothing and foods to Māori across New Zealand, in exchange for resources, land and labour. Māori began selectively adopting elements of Western society during the 19th century, including European clothing and food, and later Western education, religion and architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>But as the 19th century wore on, relations between European colonial settlers and different Māori groups became increasingly strained. Tensions led to conflict in the 1860s, and the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_land_confiscations" title="New Zealand land confiscations">confiscation of millions of acres of Māori land</a>. Significant amounts of land were also purchased by the colonial government and later through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_Land_Court" title="Native Land Court">Native Land Court</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By the start of the 20th century, a greater awareness had emerged of a unified Māori identity, particularly in comparison to Pākehā, who now overwhelmingly outnumbered the Māori as a whole. Māori and Pākehā societies remained largely separate—socially, culturally, economically and geographically—for much of the 19th and early 20th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The key reason for this was that Māori remained almost exclusively a rural population, whereas increasingly the European population was urban especially after 1900. Nevertheless, Māori groups continued to engage with the government and in legal processes to increase their standing in (and ultimately further their incorporation into) wider New Zealand society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The main point of contact with the government were the four Māori Members of Parliament.
</p><p>Many Māori <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urbanisation" title="Urbanisation">migrated to larger rural towns and cities</a> during the Depression and post-WWII periods in search of employment, leaving rural communities depleted and disconnecting many urban Māori from their traditional social controls and tribal homelands. Yet while standards of living improved among Māori, they continued to lag behind Pākehā in areas such as health, income, skilled employment and access to higher levels of education. Māori leaders and government policymakers alike struggled to deal with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_issues" title="Social issues">social issues</a> stemming from increased urban migration, including a shortage of housing and jobs, and a rise in urban crime, poverty and health problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorrenson_1997,_pp_339–41_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sorrenson_1997,_pp_339–41-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In regards to housing, a 1961 census revealed significant differences in the living conditions of Māori and Europeans. That year, out of all the (unshared) non-Māori private dwellings in New Zealand, 96.8 per cent had a bath or shower, 94.1 per cent a hot water service, 88.7 per cent a flush toilet, 81.6 per cent a refrigerator, and 78.6 per cent an electric washing machine. By contrast, for all (unshared) Māori private dwellings that same year, 76.8 per cent had a bath or shower, 68.9 per cent a hot water service, 55.8 per cent a refrigerator, 54.1 per cent a flush toilet, and 47 per cent an electric washing machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the arrival of Europeans had a profound impact on the Māori way of life, many aspects of traditional society have survived into the 21st century. Māori participate fully in all spheres of New Zealand culture and society, leading largely Western lifestyles while also maintaining their own cultural and social customs. The traditional social strata of <i>rangatira</i>, <i>tūtūā</i> and <i>mōkai</i> have all but disappeared from Māori society, while the roles of tohunga and <a href="/wiki/Kaum%C4%81tua" title="Kaumātua">kaumātua</a> are still present. Traditional kinship ties are also actively maintained, and the whānau in particular remains an integral part of Māori life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Marae.2C_hap.C5.AB_and_iwi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Marae,_hapū_and_iwi">Marae, hapū and iwi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Marae, hapū and iwi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whenuakura_Marae,_Taranaki,_New_Zealand.jpg"><img alt="Whenuakura Marae in Taranaki" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2138" data-file-width="2800" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/250px-Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/375px-Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg/500px-Whenuakura_Marae%2C_Taranaki%2C_New_Zealand.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whenuakura_Marae,_Taranaki,_New_Zealand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Whenuakura" title="Whenuakura">Whenuakura</a> Marae in Taranaki. Marae continue to function as local community centres in modern Māori society.</div></div></div>
<p>Māori society at a local level is particularly visible at the <a href="/wiki/Marae" title="Marae">marae</a>. Formerly the central meeting spaces in traditional villages, marae today usually comprise a group of buildings around an open space, that frequently host events such as weddings, funerals, church services and other large gatherings, with traditional protocol and etiquette usually observed. They also serve as the base of one or sometimes several hapū.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Māori affiliate with one or more iwi (and hapū), based on genealogical descent (<i><a href="/wiki/Whakapapa" title="Whakapapa">whakapapa</a></i>). Iwi vary in size, from a few hundred members to over 100,000 in the case of Ngāpuhi. Many people do not live in their traditional tribal regions as a result of urban migration.
</p><p>Iwi are usually governed by <a href="/wiki/R%C5%ABnanga" title="Rūnanga">rūnanga</a> (governing councils or trust boards) which represent the iwi in consultations and negotiations with the New Zealand government. Rūnanga also manage tribal assets and spearhead health, education, economic and social initiatives to help iwi members.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socioeconomic_challenges">Socioeconomic challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Socioeconomic challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Māori on average have fewer assets than the rest of the population, and run greater risks of many negative economic and social outcomes. Over 50 per cent of Māori live in areas in the three highest deprivation deciles, compared with 24 per cent of the rest of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
Although Māori make up only 14 per cent of the population, they make up almost 50 per cent of the prison population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Māori have higher unemployment-rates than other cultures resident in New Zealand <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
Māori have higher numbers of suicides than non-Māori.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
"Only 47 per cent of Māori school-leavers finish school with qualifications higher than <a href="/wiki/National_Certificate_of_Educational_Achievement" title="National Certificate of Educational Achievement">NCEA</a> Level One; compared to 74 per cent European; 87 per cent Asian."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
Although New Zealand rates very well globally in the PISA rankings that compare national performance in reading, science and maths, "once you disaggregate the PISA scores, Pakeha students are second in the world and Maori are 34th."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
Māori suffer more health problems, including higher levels of alcohol and drug abuse, smoking and obesity. Less frequent use of healthcare services mean that late diagnosis and treatment intervention lead to higher levels of morbidity and mortality in many manageable conditions, such as <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cancer" title="Cervical cancer">cervical cancer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> per head of population than non-Māori.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Although Māori life expectancy rates have increased dramatically in the last 50 years, they still have considerably lower life-expectancies compared to New Zealanders of European ancestry: in 2004, Māori males lived 69.0 years vs. non-Māori males 77.2 years; Māori females 73.2 yrs vs. non-Māori females 81.9 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> This gap had narrowed by 2013: 72.8 years for men and 76.5 years for women, compared to 80.2 years for non-Māori men and 83.7 years for non-Māori women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Also, a recent study by the New Zealand Family Violence Clearinghouse showed that Māori women and children are more likely to experience domestic violence than any other ethnic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Race_relations">Race relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Race relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hikoi_011.jpg"><img alt="Protest hikoi during the Foreshore and Seabed controversy in 2004" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="799" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hikoi_011.jpg/140px-Hikoi_011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hikoi_011.jpg/210px-Hikoi_011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Hikoi_011.jpg/280px-Hikoi_011.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hikoi_011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protest <a href="/wiki/Hikoi" title="Hikoi">hikoi</a> during the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_foreshore_and_seabed_controversy" title="New Zealand foreshore and seabed controversy">Foreshore and seabed controversy</a> in 2004.</div></div></div>
<p>The status of Māori as the indigenous people of New Zealand is recognised in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_law" title="New Zealand law">New Zealand law</a> by the term <i><a href="/wiki/Tangata_whenua" title="Tangata whenua">tangata whenua</a></i> (lit. "people of the land"), which identifies the traditional connection between Māori and a given area of land. Māori as a whole can be considered as tangata whenua of New Zealand entirely; individual iwi are recognised as tangata whenua for areas of New Zealand in which they are traditionally based, while hapū are tangata whenua within their marae. New Zealand law periodically requires consultation between the government and tangata whenua—for example, during major land development projects. This usually takes the form of negotiations between local or national government and the rūnanga of one or more relevant iwi, although the government generally decides which (if any) concerns are acted upon.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg"><img alt="New Zealand endorses Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous People, 2010" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/230px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/345px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg/460px-NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NZ_delegation_UN_Forum_on_Indigenous_Issues.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New Zealand endorses the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Rights_of_Indigenous_Peoples" title="Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples">Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples</a> in April 2010.</div></div></div>
<p>Māori issues are a prominent feature of race relations in New Zealand. Historically, many Pākehā viewed race relations in their country as being the "best in the world", a view that prevailed until Māori urban migration in the mid-20th century brought cultural and socioeconomic differences to wider attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Māori protest movements grew significantly in the 1960s and 1970s seeking redress for past grievances, particularly in regard to land rights. Successive governments have responded by enacting <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a> programmes, funding cultural rejuvenation initiatives and negotiating tribal settlements for past breaches of the Treaty of Waitangi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Further efforts have focused on cultural preservation and reducing socioeconomic disparity.
</p><p>Nevertheless, race relations remains a contentious issue in New Zealand society. Māori advocates continue to push for further redress claiming that their concerns are being marginalised or ignored. A 2007 <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Corrections_(New_Zealand)" title="Department of Corrections (New Zealand)">Department of Corrections</a> report found that Māori are disproportionately represented in the criminal justice system not only because they commit more crimes but also because they face prejudice at many levels: "a number of studies have shown evidence of greater likelihood, associated only with ethnicity, for Māori offenders to have police contact, be charged, lack legal representation, not be granted bail, plead guilty, be convicted, be sentenced to non-monetary penalties, and be denied release to Home Detention".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Conversely, critics denounce the scale of assistance given to Māori as amounting to preferential treatment for a select group of people based on race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Both sentiments were highlighted during the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_foreshore_and_seabed_controversy" title="New Zealand foreshore and seabed controversy">foreshore and seabed controversy</a> in 2004, in which the New Zealand government claimed sole ownership of the New Zealand foreshore and seabed, over the objections of Māori groups who were seeking customary title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commerce">Commerce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Commerce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Law_Commission" title="New Zealand Law Commission">New Zealand Law Commission</a> has started a project to develop a legal framework for Māori who want to manage communal resources and responsibilities. The voluntary system proposes an alternative to existing companies, incorporations, and trusts in which tribes and hapū and other groupings can interact with the legal system. The foreshadowed legislation, under the proposed name of the "Waka Umanga (Māori Corporations) Act", would provide a model adaptable to suit the needs of individual iwi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> At the end of 2009, the proposed legislation was awaiting a second hearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wider commercial exposure has increased public awareness of the Māori culture, but has also resulted in several notable legal disputes. Between 1998 and 2006, <a href="/wiki/Ng%C4%81ti_Toa" title="Ngāti Toa">Ngāti Toa</a> attempted to trademark the haka "<a href="/wiki/Ka_Mate" title="Ka Mate">Ka Mate</a>" to prevent its use by commercial organisations without their permission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> In 2001, Danish toymaker Lego faced legal action by several Māori tribal groups (fronted by lawyer Maui Solomon) and members of the on-line discussion forum Aotearoa Cafe for trademarking Māori words used in naming the Bionicle product range (<i>see <a href="/wiki/Bionicle#Māori_language_controversy" title="Bionicle">Bionicle Māori controversy</a></i>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_representation">Political representation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Political representation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_politics" title="Māori politics">Māori politics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Papawai1897.jpg"><img alt="The Māori Parliament at Pāpāwai, Greytown in 1897" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="700" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Papawai1897.jpg/250px-Papawai1897.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Papawai1897.jpg/375px-Papawai1897.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Papawai1897.jpg/500px-Papawai1897.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Papawai1897.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The opening of the Māori Parliament at Pāpāwai, <a href="/wiki/Greytown,_New_Zealand" title="Greytown, New Zealand">Greytown</a> in 1897, with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Seddon" title="Richard Seddon">Richard Seddon</a> in attendance.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1080" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg/220px-Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg/330px-Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg/440px-Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tino_Rangatiratanga_Maori_sovereignty_movement_flag.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flag of Māori proposed in 1998<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Māori have been involved in New Zealand politics since the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Independence_of_New_Zealand" title="Declaration of the Independence of New Zealand">Declaration of the Independence of New Zealand</a>, before the Treaty of Waitangi was signed in 1840. Māori have had <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_electorates" title="Māori electorates">reserved seats</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Parliament" title="New Zealand Parliament">New Zealand Parliament</a> since 1868: presently, this accounts for seven of the 122 seats in New Zealand's unicameral parliament. The contesting of these seats was the first opportunity for many Māori to participate in New Zealand elections, although the elected Māori representatives initially struggled to assert significant influence. Māori received universal suffrage with other New Zealand citizens in 1893.
</p><p>Being a traditionally tribal people, no one organisation ostensibly speaks for all Māori nationwide. The <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_King_Movement" title="Māori King Movement">Māori King Movement</a> originated in the 1860s as an attempt by several iwi to unify under one leader: in modern times, it serves a largely ceremonial role. Another attempt at political unity was the Kotahitanga Movement, which established a separate Māori Parliament that held annual sessions from 1892 until its last sitting in 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are seven designated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_seats" title="Māori seats">Māori seats</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Parliament" title="New Zealand Parliament">New Zealand Parliament</a> (and Māori can and do stand in and win general roll seats), and consideration of and consultation with Māori have become routine requirements for councils and government organisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seats_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-seats-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Debate occurs frequently as to the relevance and legitimacy of the Māori electoral roll and seats. The National Party announced in 2008 it would abolish the seats when all historic Treaty settlements have been resolved, which it aimed to complete by 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> However, after the election National reached an agreement with the Māori Party not to abolish the seats until Māori give their approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several Māori political parties have formed over the years to improve the position of Māori in New Zealand society. The present <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Party" title="Māori Party">Māori Party</a>, formed in 2004, secured 1.32 per cent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Party_vote" title="Party vote">party vote</a> at the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_general_election,_2014" title="New Zealand general election, 2014">2014 general election</a> and held two seats in the <a href="/wiki/51st_New_Zealand_Parliament" title="51st New Zealand Parliament">51st New Zealand Parliament</a>, with two MPs serving as Ministers outside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Cabinet" title="New Zealand Cabinet">Cabinet</a>. The party did not achieve any representatives in the <a href="/wiki/52nd_New_Zealand_Parliament" title="52nd New Zealand Parliament">52nd New Zealand Parliament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C4%81ori_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264625">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{list-style-type:none;margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>dl>dd{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}</style><div class="refbegin" style="">
<ul><li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Notei"><b><a href="#ref_Notei">^i</a></b></span> : <i>Māori</i> has cognates in other <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_language" title="Hawaiian language">Hawaiian</a> <i>maoli</i>, <a href="/wiki/Tahitian_language" title="Tahitian language">Tahitian</a> <i>mā'ohi</i>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cook_Islands_Maori" title="Cook Islands Maori">Cook Islands Maori</a> <i>māori</i> which all share similar meanings.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Noteii"><b><a href="#ref_Noteii">^ii</a></b></span> : The orthographic conventions developed by the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_Language_Commission" title="Māori Language Commission">Māori Language Commission</a> (<i>Te Taura Whiri i te Reo Māori</i>) recommend the use of the <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">macron</a> (ā ē ī ō ū) to denote long vowels. Contemporary English-language usage in New Zealand tends to avoid the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicise" title="Anglicise">anglicised</a> plural form of the word <i>Māori</i> with an "s": The Māori language generally marks plurals by changing the <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">article</a> rather than the noun, for example: <i>te waka</i> (the canoe); <i>ngā waka</i> (the canoes).</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Noteiii"><b><a href="#ref_Noteiii">^iii</a></b></span> : In 2003, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Cullen" title="Christian Cullen">Christian Cullen</a> became a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_M%C4%81ori_rugby_union_team" title="New Zealand Māori rugby union team">Māori rugby team</a> despite having, according to his father, about 1/64 Māori ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Noteiv"><b><a href="#ref_Noteiv">^iv</a></b></span> : Although, as noted elsewhere in this article, evidence is increasingly pointing to 1280 as the earliest date of settlement.</li></ul>
</div>
